You have Sourcegraph MCP tools available for this task. Using these tools is a **required part of solving this task** — not optional.

### REQUIRED: Use Deep Search (`mcp__sourcegraph__sg_deepsearch`)

You have access to **Deep Search** — a powerful semantic code analysis tool that traces call chains, data flows, and architectural relationships across files. It is **far more effective** than keyword search for understanding how code fits together.

**IMPORTANT: Deep Search is asynchronous.** It returns a polling link, not immediate results. Follow this workflow:

**Step 0 (BEFORE everything else):**
1. Call `mcp__sourcegraph__sg_deepsearch` with a query describing what this task requires. Examples:
   - "How does [feature/component from the task] work in this codebase?"
   - "Where is [concept from the task] implemented and what are its dependencies?"
   - "Trace the code path for [operation described in the task]"
2. You will receive a polling link back. This is expected — do NOT call `sg_deepsearch_read` immediately.
3. Instead, proceed to Step 1 below and do useful work with keyword search, list_files, and read_file for 30-60 seconds.
4. Then call `mcp__sourcegraph__sg_deepsearch_read` with the link to check for results.
5. **If it still says "Poll for results"** — continue working with other tools and try `sg_deepsearch_read` again after another 30 seconds of work.
6. You **MUST** call `sg_deepsearch_read` at least 3 times before giving up. Deep Search typically takes 50-120 seconds to complete.

When Deep Search results arrive, they contain rich semantic analysis (6-17K chars of code structure, call chains, architectural patterns). This is far more valuable than keyword search results.

**When to use Deep Search (mandatory for all of these):**
- Understanding how a feature or subsystem works end-to-end
- Tracing data flow or request lifecycle across multiple files
- Finding all the places that need to change for a cross-cutting modification
- Understanding architectural relationships before making changes
- Any time you need to understand "how does X work?" or "what calls Y?"

Do NOT skip Deep Search and jump straight to keyword search or file browsing. Do NOT give up after one polling attempt — interleave other work while waiting for results.

# Fault Localization: NFS Mount Disappears Due to Inode Revalidate Failure

- **Repository**: torvalds/linux
- **Kernel Version**: 4.1.15
- **Difficulty**: expert
- **Category**: fault_localization
- **Subsystem**: File System
- **Component**: NFS / SunRPC

## Bug Report

**Bug ID**: 117651
**Summary**: Root NFS and autofs - mount disappears due to inode revalidate failed
**Regression**: Yes (works on 3.14.12, broken on 4.1.15)

After upgrading from 3.14.12 to 4.1.15 I've got strange behaviour with autofs.

It happens on diskless machine with root on NFS. There is autofs indirect mount on /storage, where NFS volumes are mounted on demand.

Everything mounts normally, but after mount expiration (during unmount) autofs mount disappears - that is, not only /storage/<volume> unmounts, but /storage is also unmounted. This happens quite often - usually on the very first expiration.

automount logs umount error in such cases.

Without autofs, mount/umount of the same NFS volume works correctly.

With NFS debug enabled, I've got following errors:

```
NFS reply getattr: -512
nfs_revalidate_inode: (0:15/6291480) getattr failed, error=-512
NFS: nfs_lookup_revalidate(/storage) is invalid
```

After inode revalidate failure all mounts on this inode are unmounted and so autofs mount disappears, while automount daemon itself continue to run.

tcpdump shows, that server replies to getattr without error, but client doesn't see this reply, and returning error instead.

I am ready to supply additional information.

## Task

You are a fault localization agent. Given the bug report above, your task is to identify the **exact source file(s)** and **function(s)/data structure(s)** in the Linux kernel source tree (at `/workspace`) that need to be modified to fix this bug.

You must:
1. Analyze the bug report to understand the symptoms and context
2. Search the kernel source code to identify the relevant subsystem and files
3. Narrow down to the specific file(s) and function(s)/data structure(s) that contain the bug or need modification
4. Write your findings to `/workspace/fault_localization_result.json` in the following format:

```json
{
  "buggy_files": ["path/to/file.c"],
  "buggy_functions": ["function_or_struct_name"],
  "confidence": 0.8,
  "reasoning": "Brief explanation of why these locations are the fault"
}
```

**Important**: Paths should be relative to the kernel source root (e.g., `net/sunrpc/xprtsock.c`, not `/workspace/net/...`).

     1→/*
     2→ *  linux/net/sunrpc/xprt.c
     3→ *
     4→ *  This is a generic RPC call interface supporting congestion avoidance,
     5→ *  and asynchronous calls.
     6→ *
     7→ *  The interface works like this:
     8→ *
     9→ *  -	When a process places a call, it allocates a request slot if
    10→ *	one is available. Otherwise, it sleeps on the backlog queue
    11→ *	(xprt_reserve).
    12→ *  -	Next, the caller puts together the RPC message, stuffs it into
    13→ *	the request struct, and calls xprt_transmit().
    14→ *  -	xprt_transmit sends the message and installs the caller on the
    15→ *	transport's wait list. At the same time, if a reply is expected,
    16→ *	it installs a timer that is run after the packet's timeout has
    17→ *	expired.
    18→ *  -	When a packet arrives, the data_ready handler walks the list of
    19→ *	pending requests for that transport. If a matching XID is found, the
    20→ *	caller is woken up, and the timer removed.
    21→ *  -	When no reply arrives within the timeout interval, the timer is
    22→ *	fired by the kernel and runs xprt_timer(). It either adjusts the
    23→ *	timeout values (minor timeout) or wakes up the caller with a status
    24→ *	of -ETIMEDOUT.
    25→ *  -	When the caller receives a notification from RPC that a reply arrived,
    26→ *	it should release the RPC slot, and process the reply.
    27→ *	If the call timed out, it may choose to retry the operation by
    28→ *	adjusting the initial timeout value, and simply calling rpc_call
    29→ *	again.
    30→ *
    31→ *  Support for async RPC is done through a set of RPC-specific scheduling
    32→ *  primitives that `transparently' work for processes as well as async
    33→ *  tasks that rely on callbacks.
    34→ *
    35→ *  Copyright

   792→				"server %s\n", task->tk_pid, -task->tk_status,

103-assistanttext-
Let me look carefully at the call_status function and figure out the issue. When a signal arrives:

1. The task is woken with status -ERESTARTSYS (from sched.c:792)
2. call_status() is called with task->tk_status = -ERESTARTSYS
3. ERESTARTSYS is not in the switch cases, so it falls through to the default case
4. The default case calls rpc_exit(task, status) which returns -512 to NFS

The check at line 2018-2019 (`if (req->rq_reply_bytes_recvd > 0 && !req->rq_bytes_sent)`) would only override the status if bytes were already received AND no bytes were sent. But typically bytes have been sent (the GETATTR request was sent).

Let me check if there's a race condition where a signal arrives after data is in the socket buffer but before xprt_complete_rqst() is called.

Excellent! The Deep Search has provided very valuable information. According to the analysis:

**The key change is in `fs/nfs/nfs4state.c` in the `nfs4_wait_clnt_recover` function:**

- In kernel 3.14: Used `wait_on_bit()`
- In kernel 4.1: Changed to `wait_on_bit_action()`

The `nfs_wait_bit_killable` function returns -ERESTARTSYS when a fatal signal is pending, and this change causes the error to be propagated properly through the NFS client, but without being handled gracefully when the RPC reply was actually successfully received.

Let me verify this in the local code.

I see the issue! Look at line 2018:
```c
if (req->rq_reply_bytes_recvd > 0 && !req->rq_bytes_sent)
```

The condition checks `!req->rq_bytes_sent`, meaning the override only happens if NO bytes were sent. But in a typical GETATTR scenario, bytes WERE sent (the request was transmitted), so `rq_bytes_sent > 0`, and the condition fails even if `rq_reply_bytes_recvd > 0`.

This appears to be a bug: if the reply HAS been received (`rq_reply_bytes_recvd > 0`), the task should proceed to decode the reply regardless of whether bytes were sent.

Now I understand the issue. When `rpc_exit(task, -ERESTARTSYS)` is called at sched.c:792, it sets:
1. `task->tk_status = -ERESTARTSYS`
2. `task->tk_action = rpc_exit_task`

The problem is that there's no check in this path for whether data was already received. When the signal arrives:
1. The RPC reply might already be in the socket buffer (tcpdump shows it)
2. But xprt_complete_rqst() might not have been called yet
3. So rq_reply_bytes_recvd is still 0
4. The signal interrupts the wait
5. rpc_exit() is called with -ERESTARTSYS
6. The NFS layer receives -512

139-assistanttext-
Now I understand the complete picture. The bug is at `net/sunrpc/sched.c` lines 783-792. When a signal is received:

1. The task wakes up from `out_of_line_wait_on_bit` with `status == -ERESTARTSYS`
2. The code immediately calls `rpc_exit(task, -ERESTARTSYS)` without checking if the reply was already received
3. This causes the NFS layer to see -512 (ERESTARTSYS) even though the server's reply was successfully received

The fix should be to check `req->rq_reply_bytes_recvd` before calling `rpc_exit(task, -ERESTARTSYS)`. If the reply was already received, the task should continue processing normally.

Let me now write the fault localization result:

The fix should check req->rq_reply_bytes_recvd before calling rpc_exit() - if bytes have been received, the task should continue to decode the reply rather than returning the signal error."

153-assistanttext-
I have completed the fault localization task. Here's a summary:

## Fault Localization Summary

**Buggy File:** `net/sunrpc/sched.c`  
**Buggy Function:** `__rpc_execute`

### Root Cause Analysis

The bug occurs at lines 783-792 in `net/sunrpc/sched.c`:

```c
if (status == -ERESTARTSYS) {
    /*
     * When a sync task receives a signal, it exits with
     * -ERESTARTSYS. In order to catch any callbacks that
     * clean up after sleeping on some queue, we don't
     * break the loop here, but go around once more.
     */
    dprintk("RPC: %5u got signal\n", task->tk_pid);
    task->tk_flags |= RPC_TASK_KILLED;
    rpc_exit(task, -ERESTARTSYS);
}
```

When a synchronous RPC task (like NFS getattr) is waiting for a reply:
1. The server sends the reply (visible in tcpdump)
2. Before the RPC layer calls `xprt_complete_rqst()` to mark the reply as received
3. A fatal signal arrives (e.g., from autofs timeout handling)
4. The `out_of_line_wait_on_bit` returns -ERESTARTSYS
5. The code immediately calls `rpc_exit(task, -ERESTARTSYS)` without checking if `rq_reply_bytes_recvd > 0`
6. The NFS layer receives -512 (ERESTARTSYS) and the inode revalidation fails

The fix should check `req->rq_reply_bytes_recvd` before returning the signal error. If the reply has already been received, the task should continue to decode the reply rather than exiting with an error.

    "content": "/*\n *  linux/net/sunrpc/xprt.c\n *\n *  This is a generic RPC call interface supporting congestion avoidance,\n *  and asynchronous calls.\n *\n *  The interface works like this:\n *\n *  -\tWhen a process places a call, it allocates a request slot if\n *\tone is available. Otherwise, it sleeps on the backlog queue\n *\t(xprt_reserve).\n *  -\tNext, the caller puts together the RPC message, stuffs it into\n *\tthe request struct, and calls xprt_transmit().\n *  -\txprt_transmit sends the message and installs the caller on the\n *\ttransport's wait list. At the same time, if a reply is expected,\n *\tit installs a timer that is run after the packet's timeout has\n *\texpired.\n *  -\tWhen a packet arrives, the data_ready handler walks the list of\n *\tpending requests for that transport. If a matching XID is found, the\n *\tcaller is woken up, and the timer removed.\n *  -\tWhen no reply arrives within the timeout interval, the timer is\n *\tfired by the kernel and runs xprt_timer(). It either adjusts the\n *\ttimeout values (minor timeout) or wakes up the caller with a status\n *\tof -ETIMEDOUT.\n *  -\tWhen the caller receives a notification from RPC that a reply arrived,\n *\tit should release the RPC slot, and process the reply.\n *\tIf the call timed out, it may choose to retry the operation by\n *\tadjusting the initial timeout value, and simply calling rpc_call\n *\tagain.\n *\n *  Support for async RPC is done through a set of RPC-specific scheduling\n *  primitives that `transparently' work for processes as well as async\n *  tasks that rely on callbacks.\n *\n *  Copyright (C) 1995-1997, Olaf Kirch <okir@monad.swb.de>\n *\n *  Transport switch API copyright (C) 2005, Chuck Lever <cel@netapp.com>\n */\n\n#include <linux/module.h>\n\n#include <linux/types.h>\n#include <linux/interrupt.h>\n#include <linux/workqueue.h>\n#include <linux/net.h>\n#include <linux/ktime.h>\n\n#include <linux/sunrpc/clnt.h>\n#include <linux/sunrpc/metrics.h>\n#include <linux/sunrpc/bc_xprt.h>\n\n#include <trace/events/sunrpc.h>\n\n#include \"sunrpc.h\"\n\n/*\n * Local variables\n */\n\n#if IS_ENABLED(CONFIG_SUNRPC_DEBUG)\n# define RPCDBG_FACILITY\tRPCDBG_XPRT\n#endif\n\n/*\n * Local functions\n */\nstatic void\t xprt_init(struct rpc_xprt *xprt, struct net *net);\nstatic void\txprt_request_init(struct rpc_task *, struct rpc_xprt *);\nstatic void\txprt_connect_status(struct rpc_task *task);\nstatic int      __xprt_get_cong(struct rpc_xprt *, struct rpc_task *);\nstatic void\t xprt_destroy(struct rpc_xprt *xprt);\n\nstatic DEFINE_SPINLOCK(xprt_list_lock);\nstatic LIST_HEAD(xprt_list);\n\n/**\n * xprt_register_transport - struct nfs4_exception exception = { };\\n    int err;\\n    do {\\n        err = _nfs4_proc_getattr(server, fhandle, fattr, label);\\n        err = nfs4_handle_exception(server, err, &exception);\\n [include/linux/errno.h:13](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux@v4.1/-/blob/include/linux/errno.h?L13) as error code 512. According to the comments:\\n> \\\"These should never be seen by user programs. To return one of ERESTART* codes, signal_pending() MUST be set.\\\"\\n\\n### 4. **nfs4_handle_exception** ([fs/nfs/nfs4proc.c:347-449](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux@v4.1/-/blob/fs/nfs/nfs4proc.c?L347-449))\\n\\nThis function handles NFSv4 error recovery. At line 420, it calls `nfs4_delay()` for certain errors, and at line 443, it calls **`nfs4_wait_clnt_recover()`** for recovery scenarios.\\n\\n## The Critical Change Between 3.14 and 4.1\\n\\nThe key difference is in **[fs/nfs/nfs4state.c](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux/-/blob/fs/nfs/nfs4state.c)**:\\n\\n### Version 3.14 (lines 1254-1255):\\n```c\\nres = wait_on_bit(&clp->cl_state, NFS4CLNT_MANAGER_RUNNING,\\n                  nfs_wait_bit_killable, TASK_KILLABLE);\\n```\\n\\n### Version 4.1 (lines 1248-1249):\\n```c\\nres = wait_on_bit_action(&clp->cl_state, NFS4CLNT_MANAGER_RUNNING,\\n                         nfs_wait_bit_killable, TASK_KILLABLE);\\n```\\n\\n**The change from `wait_on_bit()` to `wait_on_bit_action()`** is significant.\\n\\n### nfs_wait_bit_killable ([fs/nfs/inode.c:78-84](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux@v4.1/-/blob/fs/nfs/inode.c?L78-84)):\\n```c\\nint nfs_wait_bit_killable(struct wait_bit_key *key)\\n{\\n    if (fatal_signal_pending(current))\\n        return -ERESTARTSYS;\\n    freezable_schedule_unsafe();\\n    return 0;\\n}\\n```\\n\\n## Root Cause\\n\\nWhen a process is waiting for the NFS4 state manager to complete (e.g., during lease recovery, session recovery, or migration):\\n\\n1. **In kernel 3.14**: `wait_on_bit()` would call `nfs_wait_bit_killable()` but the return value handling was different\\n2. **In kernel 4.1**: `wait_on_bit_action()` properly propagates the return value from `nfs_wait_bit_killable()`\\n\\nIf a **fatal signal** arrives while waiting (line 80 checks `fatal_signal_pending()`), the function returns **-ERESTARTSYS**.\\n\\nThis gets propagated through:\\n1. `nfs4_wait_clnt_recover()` \u2192 returns -ERESTARTSYS\\n2. `nfs4_handle_exception()` \u2192 returns -ERESTARTSYS (via `nfs4_map_errors()` at line 441)\\n3. `nfs4_proc_getattr()` \u2192 returns -ERESTARTSYS\\n4. `__nfs_revalidate_inode()` \u2192 returns -ERESTARTSYS to caller\\n\\n**Even though the RPC completed successfully on the wire**, if the client was waiting for state recovery and received a fatal signal, it returns ERESTARTSYS instead of the successful server response.\\n\\n## Why This is Problematic\\n\\nThe issue occurs when:\\n- NFS client is recovering state (session recovery, lease renewal, etc.)\\n- A getattr operation needs to wait for this recovery to complete\\n- A fatal signal (e.g., SIGKILL) is delivered to the process\\n- The wait is interrupted with -ERESTARTSYS **even if the server reply was successful**\\n\\nThis can lead to false failures where operations appear to fail from the client's perspective when the server actually processed them successfully.\\n\\n## Specifically, I need to understand the code path for nfs_revalidate_inode, nfs_lookup_revalidate, and how getattr operations returning error -512 (ERESTARTSYS) are handled. What changed between kernel 3.14 and 4.1 that could cause getattr to return -512 when the server actually replied successfully?\n\nPerfect! Now I have a comprehensive understanding of the issue. Let me create a summary response:\n\nBased on my analysis of the Linux kernel NFS code in versions 3.14 and 4.1, I can explain how NFS inode revalidation works and identify the key change that could cause getattr to return ERESTARTSYS (-512) when the server replied successfully.\n\n## NFS Inode Revalidation Code Path\n\n### 1. **nfs_revalidate_inode** ([fs/nfs/inode.c:929-996](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux@v4.1/-/blob/fs/nfs/inode.c?L929-996))\n\nThis is the main entry point for inode revalidation:\n- Calls `NFS_PROTO(inode)->getattr()` to fetch attributes from the server (line 960)\n- For NFSv4, this maps to `nfs4_proc_getattr()`\n- Returns the status from getattr, which may be ERESTARTSYS\n\n### 2. **nfs_lookup_revalidate** ([fs/nfs/dir.c:1112-1230](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux@v4.1/-/blob/fs/nfs/dir.c?L1112-1230))\n\nFor directory entry revalidation:\n- Calls `NFS_PROTO(dir)->lookup()` to verify the dentry is still valid (line 1183)\n- If the lookup fails with any error, including ERESTARTSYS, it marks the dentry as invalid\n\n### 3. **nfs4_proc_getattr** ([fs/nfs/nfs4proc.c:3221-3233](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux@v4.1/-/blob/fs/nfs/nfs4proc.c?L3221-3233))\n\nThe NFSv4 getattr implementation:\n```c\nstatic int nfs4_proc_getattr(struct nfs_server *server, struct nfs_fh *fhandle,\n                            struct nfs_fattr *fattr, struct nfs4_label *label)\n{\n    struct nfs4_exception exception = { };\n    int err;\n    do {\n        err = _nfs4_proc_getattr(server, fhandle, fattr, label);\n        err = nfs4_handle_exception(server, err, &exception);\n    } while (exception.retry);\n    return err;\n}\n```\n\nThis wraps the actual RPC call with exception handling and retry logic.\n\n## ERESTARTSYS (-512) Handling\n\nERESTARTSYS is defined in [include/linux/errno.h:13](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux@v4.1/-/blob/include/linux/errno.h?L13) as error code 512. According to the comments:\n> \"These should never be seen by user programs. To return one of ERESTART* codes, signal_pending() MUST be set.\"\n\n### 4. **nfs4_handle_exception** ([fs/nfs/nfs4proc.c:347-449](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux@v4.1/-/blob/fs/nfs/nfs4proc.c?L347-449))\n\nThis function handles NFSv4 error recovery. At line 420, it calls `nfs4_delay()` for certain errors, and at line 443, it calls **`nfs4_wait_clnt_recover()`** for recovery scenarios.\n\n## The Critical Change Between 3.14 and 4.1\n\nThe key difference is in **[fs/nfs/nfs4state.c](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux/-/blob/fs/nfs/nfs4state.c)**:\n\n### Version 3.14 (lines 1254-1255):\n```c\nres = wait_on_bit(&clp->cl_state, NFS4CLNT_MANAGER_RUNNING,\n                  nfs_wait_bit_killable, TASK_KILLABLE);\n```\n\n### Version 4.1 (lines 1248-1249):\n```c\nres = wait_on_bit_action(&clp->cl_state, NFS4CLNT_MANAGER_RUNNING,\n                         nfs_wait_bit_killable, TASK_KILLABLE);\n```\n\n**The change from `wait_on_bit()` to `wait_on_bit_action()`** is significant.\n\n### nfs_wait_bit_killable ([fs/nfs/inode.c:78-84](https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/r/github.com/torvalds/linux@v4.1/-/blob/fs/nfs/inode.c?L78-84)):\n```c\nint nfs_wait_bit_killable(struct wait_bit_key *key)\n{\n    if (fatal_signal_pending(current))\n        return -ERESTARTSYS;\n    freezable_schedule_unsafe();\n    return 0;\n}\n```\n\n## Root Cause\n\nWhen a process is waiting for the NFS4 state manager to complete (e.g., during lease recovery, session recovery, or migration):\n\n1. **In kernel 3.14**: `wait_on_bit()` would call `nfs_wait_bit_killable()` but the return value handling was different\n2. **In kernel 4.1**: `wait_on_bit_action()` properly propagates the return value from `nfs_wait_bit_killable()`\n\nIf a **fatal signal** arrives while waiting (line 80 checks `fatal_signal_pending()`), the function returns **-ERESTARTSYS**.\n\nThis gets propagated through:\n1. `nfs4_wait_clnt_recover()` \u2192 returns -ERESTARTSYS\n2. `nfs4_handle_exception()` \u2192 returns -ERESTARTSYS (via `nfs4_map_errors()` at line 441)\n3. `nfs4_proc_getattr()` \u2192 returns -ERESTARTSYS\n4. `__nfs_revalidate_inode()` \u2192 returns -ERESTARTSYS to caller\n\n**Even though the RPC completed successfully on the wire**, if the client was waiting for state recovery and received a fatal signal, it returns ERESTARTSYS instead of the successful server response.\n\n## Why This is Problematic\n\nThe issue occurs when:\n- NFS client is recovering state (session recovery, lease renewal, etc.)\n- A getattr operation needs to wait for this recovery to complete\n- A fatal signal (e.g., SIGKILL) is delivered to the process\n- The wait is interrupted with -ERESTARTSYS **even if the server reply was successful**\n\nThis can lead to false failures where operations appear to fail from the client's perspective when the server actually processed them successfully.\n\n## Sources\n\n- When a synchronous RPC task receives a fatal signal while waiting for a reply (via out_of_line_wait_on_bit), the code immediately calls rpc_exit(task, -ERESTARTSYS) without first checking if the RPC reply has already been received (rq_reply_bytes_recvd). The bug report shows that tcpdump captures the server's reply arriving successfully, but the client returns -512 (ERESTARTSYS) anyway. This is because the signal interrupts the wait loop before xprt_complete_rqst() is called to set rq_reply_bytes_recvd, and the code at line 792 unconditionally exits with the signal error. The fix should check req->rq_reply_bytes_recvd before calling rpc_exit() - if bytes have been received, the task should continue to decode the reply rather than returning the signal error.\"\n}\n",
